  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer1928541622422748312"><i>An alternate-cover edition for this ISBN can be found <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40899393-the-member-of-the-wedding' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>.</i><br/><br/>The novel that became an award-winning play and a major motion picture and that has charmed generations of readers, Carson McCullers's classic <i>The Member of the Wedding</i> is now available in small-format trade paperback for the first time. Here is the story of the inimitable twelve-year-old Frankie, who is utterly, h</span>
  <span id="freeText1928541622422748312" style="display:none"><i>An alternate-cover edition for this ISBN can be found <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40899393-the-member-of-the-wedding" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</i><br /><br />The novel that became an award-winning play and a major motion picture and that has charmed generations of readers, Carson McCullers's classic <i>The Member of the Wedding</i> is now available in small-format trade paperback for the first time. Here is the story of the inimitable twelve-year-old Frankie, who is utterly, hopelessly bored with life until she hears about her older brother's wedding. Bolstered by lively conversations with her house servant, Berenice, and her six-year-old male cousin—not to mention her own unbridled imagination—Frankie takes on an overly active role in the wedding, hoping even to go, uninvited, on the honeymoon, so deep is her desire to be the member of something larger, more accepting than herself. "A marvelous study of the agony of adolescence" (<i>Detroit Free Press</i>), <i>The Member of the Wedding</i> showcases Carson McCullers at her most sensitive, astute, and lasting best.</span>

<a href="/questions/1431700-did-i-miss-what-happened-in-the-garage">Did I miss what happened in the garage?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1431700'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F330244.The_Member_of_the_Wedding">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1431700-did-i-miss-what-happened-in-the-garage">Add your answer</a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/60445861-olivia-colville"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1554875511i/60445861._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/60445861-olivia-colville">Olivia Colville</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    It was never explicitly stated, but the context leads the reader to believe that it was some kind of minor sexual/physical encounter. Frankie doesn't <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    It was never explicitly stated, but the context leads the reader to believe that it was some kind of minor sexual/physical encounter. Frankie doesn't really understand what happened (or why), which is probably why the author leaves it unspecific. She is very naive. Whatever happened is something that she feels was wrong, but she doesn't know exactly why. She now hates the boy that it happened with. There is a moment near the very end of the book when she finally links together all of the things she knows about men and women (when she saw the boarders having sex, which she thought was a "fit," what the soldier wanted to do to her in the bedroom above the bar, the "nasty things" that the girls in the club had said about married people, and what had happened in the garage). Inasmuch as this is a coming-of-age novel, this revelation is part of her maturation; and before she comes to this realization, there is a major tension between her height and mature appearance and her immature understanding of adult relationships. This is especially apparent with the soldier, when he is looking at her in a way that she can't understand and speaking to her in a "double-speak" that she can't seem to grasp.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23324271?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23324271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9797910775426115617"><b>Totally new review, after a 2020 reread</b>:<br/><br/>Remember the oppressive boredom of a long and sultry teenage summer?<br/>Feeling vaguely fearful and fiercely moody, at the precipice of childhood’s end.<br/>Wanting to be alone, grown, and babied too.<br/>Unsure whether to belong or be different.<br/>Everything in flux, including your very sense of self.<br/> <br/>This short novel will take you back to that time.<br/><br/>“<i>This was the summer when for a long time she had not been a member…<br/>It was the summer of fear for Frankie...<br/>The summer when </i></span>
  <span id="freeText9797910775426115617" style="display:none"><b>Totally new review, after a 2020 reread</b>:<br><br>Remember the oppressive boredom of a long and sultry teenage summer?<br>Feeling vaguely fearful and fiercely moody, at the precipice of childhood’s end.<br>Wanting to be alone, grown, and babied too.<br>Unsure whether to belong or be different.<br>Everything in flux, including your very sense of self.<br> <br>This short novel will take you back to that time.<br><br>“<i>This was the summer when for a long time she had not been a member…<br>It was the summer of fear for Frankie...<br>The summer when Frankie was sick and tired of being Frankie...<br>The year when Frankie thought about the world.</i>”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583683554i/29075571.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Belonging - and not, by johnhain/Pixabay</i> (<a href="https://www.psychologytoday.com/gb/blog/understand-other-people/201809/the-danger-in-belonging" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br><b>Mood(y) and characters</b><br><br>This is a book of mood and transition more than plot. Of vivid, believable, quirky characters. <br><br>It covers a few days in the life of 12-year old tomboy Frankie. It’s set in the southern US, in the early 1940s. Frankie’s father owns a watch, clock, and jewellery shop. They’re white, not well off, but not poor either. They don’t own a car, but employ Berenice (who is "colored") as a cook, housekeeper, and childminder. Six-year old cousin John Henry is often around: bright for his age and both a comfort and annoyance to Frankie. <br><br>Her older brother, Jarvis, is returning after two years away with the army, to marry Janice. Suddenly, everything changes for Frankie - including the name she uses: F Jasmine, and finally Frances: one in each section of the book. She has some similarities with Scout in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23326617" rel="nofollow noopener">To Kill a Mockingbird</a>, another motherless tomboy in the deep south, albeit slightly younger.<br><br>Frankie becomes obsessed: wanting to be a part of not just the wedding, but the married life of her brother and the bride she has yet to meet. <br>“<i>They are the we of me.</i>”<br><br>In a subtle masterstroke of not belonging, the eponymous wedding happens offstage: we see the buildup and the aftermath, with only a few retrospective details of the event itself. <br><br>“<i>The wedding was like a dream outside her power or like a show unmanaged by her in which she was supposed to have no part.</i>”<br><br><b>Passing through the valley of the shadow of <s>death</s> sex</b><br><br>“<i>There was an uneasy doubt that she could not quite place or name.</i>”<br><br>Frankie’s mother died giving birth to her, and she’s known other people who died. But it’s the shadow of puberty, sexuality, and sex that lingers more darkly and puzzlingly. The pain of no longer being able to share her father’s bed. Misunderstandings of things seen and heard. A misread and potentially dangerous situation made me shudder with fear over many - familiar - pages. <br><br>A wedding is a happy occasion, but the mood of the story is heavy with increasing foreboding, albeit sprinkled with touching moments and gentle humour. The final tragedy is unexpected, unnecessary, and powerful.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583683554i/29075572._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Taylor Flanagan as Frankie, Chelsea Manasseri as Berenice, and Jago Mystiek as John Henry. Photo by by Errich Petersen</i> (<a href="https://ctxlivetheatre.com/news/20171023-cast-photos-by-errich-petersen-a-member-o/" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br><b>Radical</b><br><br>Segregation is “the unseen line”, but neither McCullers nor her characters think “colored” people should be separated and there’s a very matter-of-fact and accepting conversation about a cross-dressing gay man (using terminology of the time).<br><br>At the last supper before the wedding, Berenice and Frankie put the world to rights and get existential. Berenice would have “no separate colored people”, with everyone having light brown skin, blue eyes (like her glass eye), and black hair. And no war. Frankie wants material things like planes and motorcycles for everyone, “a better law of gravity” (I love that!), and for people to be able “instantly change back and forth from boys to girls”. How radical is that for 1946?<br><br><b>Multisensory</b><br><br>It’s words on a page, but vividly so. Pages permeated by music (as all McCullers’ works are - she was offered a place to study piano at Juilliard). Here, it’s the recurring “jazz spangle” of a piano-tuner in a neighbours’ house, leaving melodies unfinished. <br><br>I’m sure I’d know the fragrance of Sweet Serenade if I ever encountered it. And bridging words, visuals, and scent, is lavender: usually an aromatic plant, but here, the colour of lips, ears, sky, and an evening. <br><br><b>Relishing revisiting</b><br><br>I first read this poignant novella about a tomboy near-teen, when I was a tomboy teen: desperately wanting to be adult, but wary too. Longing to belong, but also yearning to be uniquely myself, if I could only figure out what that was. <br><br>I’ve read it at least a couple of times since, and again, now my own child is beyond a teen. It’s all so true, even though it’s set in a time and place I’ve never been. <br><br>This was the first McCullers I read, and one of the first American books I read. A schoolfriend, who is still a close friend, suggested it, and I went on to read all McCullers’ other books more than once. Catherine, thank you.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583683554i/29075573._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Carson McCullers</i> (<a href="https://alexjoyner.com/2018/02/19/observing-carson-mccullers-day/" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br><b>Quotes</b><br><br>•	“The summer was like a green sick dream, or like a silent crazy jungle under glass.”<br><br>•	“The sound of whistling, and it was a grieving August song that did not end. The minutes were very long.”<br><br>•	“The lavender sky had at last grown dark and there was slanted starlight and twisted shade.”<br><br>•	“Noises at twilight had a blurred sound, and they lingered.”<br><br>•	“Their voices bloomed like flowers… She had the feeling that unknown words were in her throat, and she was ready to speak them. Strange words were flowering in her throat and now was the time for her to name them.”<br><br>•	“The trees were poison green. There was a jellied stillness in the air.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="9797910775426115617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2221694386" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2221694386" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2221694386?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=southern">southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=500-great-books-women">500-great-books-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2221694386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer459166933992324647">As my reading tastes expand, I attempt to read books by women authors from across the globe, both contemporary and classic. My reading journey until now had never included the work of Carson McCullers, even though my mother has been urging me to read her books for years. When a few friends from the reading for pleasure group said that they were doing a buddy read of McCullers' Member of a Wedding, I was pushed to join them. While not as highly regarded as her definitive work The Heart is a Lonel</span>
  <span id="freeText459166933992324647" style="display:none">As my reading tastes expand, I attempt to read books by women authors from across the globe, both contemporary and classic. My reading journey until now had never included the work of Carson McCullers, even though my mother has been urging me to read her books for years. When a few friends from the reading for pleasure group said that they were doing a buddy read of McCullers' Member of a Wedding, I was pushed to join them. While not as highly regarded as her definitive work The Heart is a Lonely Hunter, Member of a Wedding is McCullers' tale of coming of age in the south in the 1940s, a story of a misfit girl in need of guidance to navigate the world. This touching story has generated many discussions in our buddy read and one that I am grateful to have joined.<br /><br />Frankie Addams is twelve years old and has grown up in an unnamed rural community in Georgia. She reminds me of an older Scout Finch, yet less informed before her father is for the most part absent from her life. Like Scout, Frankie comes from a family where her mother died in childbirth, leaving her father a widower who never remarries. It is up to the family's colored housekeeper Berenice to guide Frankie through her childhood, and, while Berenice is always around to provide hugs and cookies, she has left Frankie largely unaware of the world at large. At the cusp of puberty, Frankie remains naïve to the changes about to happen to her and still runs wild with her younger cousin John Henry West. Having no female friends or family members to teach her about the birds and the bees, Frankie is perfectly cast as the town's misfit, while Berenice prays that a girl best friend enters her life sooner than later.<br /><br />It is in this context that we meet twelve year old Frankie in the August of her brother's wedding as she plots to leave her home and join her brother on a worldwide adventure through life. She tells her father and Berenice and anyone who will listen that when the family goes to the wedding in Winter Hill, that she will not be coming back. Naturally because her brother's name is Jarvis and his bride to be is named Janice, Frankie decides to call herself Jasmine, making her a natural member of the JA club. She believes that Jarvis and Janice will adopt her and take her with them wherever they go, making her a member of their wedding. This plotting leads Frankie to buy a lewd dress for the occasion that John Henry dubs a Christmas tree and has her gallivanting through town on adventures as she is restless and has no adult guidance in her life. While Berenice teases her for her choice of gown, she does little to stop Frankie from exploring her community, even when she is on the verge of grave danger. The father just nods when Frankie says she is not coming back, because either he does not believe this to be true, or he is so removed from his daughter's life that he does not know what goes on at his home on a daily basis. With the home life being what it was, I almost wanted Frankie to leave town, even though I knew that this was not a feasible possibility.<br /><br />McCullers can flat out write. Her style is simple and soothing that has the reader reeled in from the opening paragraphs. Yet, the writing is also insightful as she creates multi layered characters and creates a place for them in the world at large. In many meaningful exchanges between Frankie and Berenice, McCullers has Frankie asking her housekeeper if the reason why she does not have many opportunities available before her is due to her skin color. This is wise beyond a twelve year old's years and also ahead of her time for the south during the Jim Crow era. McCullers lived some of her adult life in New York and may have been influenced by northern life. As a result, she has inserted forward thinking characters into a largely backward thinking southern town. While the two do not entirely mesh, McCullers has spun a soothing, southern tale that takes readers back to simpler times.<br /><br />My friends in this buddy read listened to an audio version narrated by Susan Sarandon. While I did not have the audio on hand, I envisioned how Sarandon would speak various portions of the novel, adding an extra layer to the beauty of this book. Although I still have not read McCullers' definitive work, I thoroughly enjoyed The Member of the Wedding. This novel allowed me to end my reading year on a positive note as I enjoyed my time with this classic southern author and made for compelling discussions in our small buddy read group. While McCullers' other novels do not feature Frankie Addams, I am looking forward to spending more time with this gifted novelist in the years to come.<br /><br />4.5 stars </span>
  <a data-text-id="459166933992324647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12485459553748460074" style="display:none">Audiobook....narrated by Susan Sarandon<br /><br />Listening to Susan Sarandon was as delicious a cuppa warm hot cocoa with baby marshmallows—her voice was comforting -soothing &amp; cozy. <br /><br />“The Heart is The Lonely Hunter” is one of my favorite books and movie”.....<br />It was wonderful to enjoy Carson McCullers writing again.<br /><br />This is a beautiful and sad story told through the eyes of a 12 year old girl - who doesn’t feel as if she fits right in her own skin. <div id="review_2572394577" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2572394577" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32804270-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564311252p2/32804270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2572394577?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=recs">recs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2572394577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16242873480234840501">Searing and sad, <i>The Member of the Wedding</i> is a deceptively simple coming-of-age tale set in the South during the midst of WWII. Frankie’s a twelve-year-old white tomboy on the cusp of thirteen who wants nothing more than to run away from her distant father and live with her soon-to-be-married brother and his partner. Motherless and excluded from other girls’ activities, she spends almost all her summer inside her home’s kitchen conversing with her six-year-old cousin John Henry and her family’s</span>
  <span id="freeText16242873480234840501" style="display:none">Searing and sad, <i>The Member of the Wedding</i> is a deceptively simple coming-of-age tale set in the South during the midst of WWII. Frankie’s a twelve-year-old white tomboy on the cusp of thirteen who wants nothing more than to run away from her distant father and live with her soon-to-be-married brother and his partner. Motherless and excluded from other girls’ activities, she spends almost all her summer inside her home’s kitchen conversing with her six-year-old cousin John Henry and her family’s Black maid Berenice. Experimental in structure the novel alternates between the trio’s hypnotic conversations about existential dread, love, and belonging and scenes sketching the protagonist’s time spent outside the home alone or around a sleazy soldier stationed in the town. A caustic sense of wit laces the fairly uneventful story, and haunting, visceral imagery abounds. The work considers different responses to the pain of social marginality, and ends with a bleak twist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16242873480234840501" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_9678659" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9678659" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9678659?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Return to Send Her</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      Loyd, I'm ready to be heartbroken
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9678659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15386437514283657730">Carson McCuller's <i>The Member of the Wedding</i> is my unrequited love story in my stable of hos: those lyrically intimate classical works I've read that stayed with me because they were confiders of sorts, someones I could go to and find some sort of explanation inside, a relating that was more than good enough of itself. (And I get my belt when they don't put out for me.) (I don't wanna say cathartic because this book isn't like that. It's often uncomfortably painful in the don't-wanna-be-reminded-</span>
  <span id="freeText15386437514283657730" style="display:none">Carson McCuller's <i>The Member of the Wedding</i> is my unrequited love story in my stable of hos: those lyrically intimate classical works I've read that stayed with me because they were confiders of sorts, someones I could go to and find some sort of explanation inside, a relating that was more than good enough of itself. (And I get my belt when they don't put out for me.) (I don't wanna say cathartic because this book isn't like that. It's often uncomfortably painful in the don't-wanna-be-reminded-of-that-wasn't-I-reading-to-forget-that-in-the-first-place way.) I collected them, books like Betty Smith's <i>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</i>, and Salinger's <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>, and held them in reserve when it got to be too much. A lot of my frames of reference in experience are heavily tied into these stories. Yeah, I was angsty at times (coughs). I'm really still doing that confusion thing (ahem), and what I read tomorrow could end up sticking with me for the rest of my days. Still, I've yet to read anything else that quite stands out to me as much when I think about unrequited love. Not the love you can sustain on your own, but the emptiness inside that needs another half to become whole. Frankie is worried about herself because there's that part missing she doesn't even know how to fill. Young Frankie is the girl throwing all of her hopes onto one thing, although the chance of it working out well are none (why would her brother and his new wife take her with them after their wedding? Doesn't she know only babies and cute puppies get adopted? Doesn't matter, it could stand in for any impossible dream). I could relate to that feeling of constantly doing the wrong thing, constantly looking in the wrong places. I can't forget about her desperation. It wasn't do what you gotta do bravery, but last chances sickness. I love Carson McCullers for capturing so well that raw feeling of clinging to make believe. The especially hard times when the weight of it becomes too much. The moments in life when the usual getting by is no longer enough... I read this for the first time when I was fourteen and then again in 2007. Both times it provoked strong feelings in me.<br />P.s. I heart Bernice. I'd have loved to have had those kitchen conversations with her. Because of her this is not a useless feel-bad book but a helps over the rough times book. Like a great conversation when all you'd had was droning voices.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15386437514283657730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1301030479" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1301030479" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5304930-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603654425p2/5304930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1301030479?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/5304930-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553250510" href="/book/show/702459.The_Member_of_the_Wedding">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5304930-mary?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5304930-mary?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1301030479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17591095066634368254"><i>She was afraid of these things that made her suddenly wonder who she was, and what she was going to be in the world, and why she was standing at that minute, seeing a light, or listening, or staring up into the sky: alone.</i><br/><br/>I read a lot about loneliness. Overwhelmingly, the books that I gravitate towards seem to have at least some thread of loneliness. But this...this didn't just deal with loneliness, this was trying to explain absolute aloneness. That completely exposed and silent and almost pani</span>
  <span id="freeText17591095066634368254" style="display:none"><i>She was afraid of these things that made her suddenly wonder who she was, and what she was going to be in the world, and why she was standing at that minute, seeing a light, or listening, or staring up into the sky: alone.</i><br /><br />I read a lot about loneliness. Overwhelmingly, the books that I gravitate towards seem to have at least some thread of loneliness. But this...this didn't just deal with loneliness, this was trying to explain absolute aloneness. That completely exposed and silent and almost panicked feeling of being just you, by yourself, in a world full of “we,” part of nothing and no one. I was a child who was alone a lot, and there were passages in here that were painfully realistic to me. So many dusks in darkened backyards with the distant sounds of people’s dishes clinking, a dog barking, other kid's voices, and maybe a faraway radio or car horn, and none of it is yours. <br /><br />This little girl narrator stood around, walked around, sat around. She wanted out. She needed someone, almost anyone. She looked up from the twilight into lighted house windows and peered into doorways, searching. She was selfish and mean with flashes of anger, but that anger made so much sense to me. She’s stuck and we’re all stuck, and damned if I of all people don’t know just how futile it can turn out to be to leave a place and start over and find that distant dog barks and car horns are everywhere, especially at dusk.<br /><br />This book surprised me. I liked it more than Lonely Hunter, perhaps because I related to it on a deeper level, but even the writing felt more intimate to me, more tortured and dreamy and in parts, philosophical. As others have pointed out, the ending feels oddly tacked-on and almost ruins the perfect tension in the first 90% of the book. Almost.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17591095066634368254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_110121586" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110121586" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-in-college">read-in-college</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=american-southern">american-southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=re-read-2014">re-read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=classics">classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110121586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4880698746852460693">Have you ever picked up a book you are certain you have read before and found that nothing feels familiar on reading it again. Of course the first time I read it was for a college course in 1967 so there may be a valid reason aside from lost brain cells...simple time or perhaps short cuts for class. But when I reached almost the very end of the book, one plot point did seem familiar and now my doubts about truly having read it are gone.<br/><br/>As for the book itself, Frankie/ F. Jasmine/ Frances is a wo</span>
  <span id="freeText4880698746852460693" style="display:none">Have you ever picked up a book you are certain you have read before and found that nothing feels familiar on reading it again. Of course the first time I read it was for a college course in 1967 so there may be a valid reason aside from lost brain cells...simple time or perhaps short cuts for class. But when I reached almost the very end of the book, one plot point did seem familiar and now my doubts about truly having read it are gone.<br /><br />As for the book itself, Frankie/ F. Jasmine/ Frances is a wonderful creation. Twelve, going on forever, she is struggling to understand who she is, what her world is and where she belongs in the world as a very large whole. Over the course of a few days, and followed by a short coda, we watch her fight with herself, her family, her housekeeper who is more than that, almost the whole town as she wages the battle with growing up. Of course she doesn't know what the battle is and that is one of the beauties of this novel...Frankie's inability to articulate even to herself what is wrong. She has one demand/desire: to go away with her brother and his new bride after the wedding. To escape her life. Everyone else knows what the outcome of that demand will be.<br /><br />There are episodes of beautiful prose throughout the novel that capture memories of summer and heat so well.<br /><br /><br /> <i>Time in August could be divided into four parts:<br /> morning, afternoon, twilight, and dark. At twilight the<br /> sky became a curious blue-green which soon faded to<br /> white. The air was soft gray, and the arbor and trees<br /> were slowly darkening. It was the hour when sparrows<br /> gathered and whirled above the rooftops of the town, <br /> and when in the darkened elms along the street there<br /> was the August sound of the cicadas. Noises at twilight<br /> had a blurred sound, and they lingered: the slam of a <br /> screen door down the street, voices of children, the<br /> whir of a lawnmower from a yard somewhere.</i><br /> (loc 1567)<br /><br /><br />This reminds me of summers of my childhood. The only stylistic negative for me, and this may be personal nitpicking, was the degree of repetitiveness in parts of the storytelling. For me at times the story stood a bit still. Then at some point the forward movement picked up and did not stop (even when Frankie continued to be in the kitchen with Berenice). <br /><br />Lastly I found Berenice to be a wonderfully realized character who could be a mother-substitute, a voice of insight on another race and way of life, a voice of reason for a girl child approaching her teen years.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4880698746852460693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1013410623" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1013410623" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1013410623?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182827" href="/book/show/855233.The_Member_of_the_Wedding">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=bechdel-pass">bechdel-pass</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=500gbw">500gbw</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1013410623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12400722546488279831">Frankie is a white girl who lives in a small Southern town and dreams of Alaska. She is twelve years old and in the throes of unbelonging. Her father has told her she’s too old to sleep in his bed, her age mates have formed groups that don’t include her, and her only day-to-day company is her six-year old cousin John Henry and middle-aged Berenice who keeps house. Her much older brother visits home with his fiancee and then goes away again to complete the wedding preparations. Frankie, who will </span>
  <span id="freeText12400722546488279831" style="display:none">Frankie is a white girl who lives in a small Southern town and dreams of Alaska. She is twelve years old and in the throes of unbelonging. Her father has told her she’s too old to sleep in his bed, her age mates have formed groups that don’t include her, and her only day-to-day company is her six-year old cousin John Henry and middle-aged Berenice who keeps house. Her much older brother visits home with his fiancee and then goes away again to complete the wedding preparations. Frankie, who will attend the wedding, is intensely captivated by the event to come, to the point of obsession. Carson McCullers, being a genius, renders this banal situation with the nightmare texture of an agonised crisis of becoming. Frankie’s unheimlich experience of her ordinary, monotonous life seems perpetually liminal. Even when she joyously enters the centre, connecting with all around her through the bridge of the wedding (at last a part of her life that others can relate to, a generic topic), she strays to the unsafe edges, she exaggerates, she lies.<br /><br />Frankie is an unlikeable protagonist; she is awkward, self-centred and selfish, gloomy, given to melodrama, unfeeling and sometimes manipulative towards others. I recognise her totally and terribly. I was just like this when I was twelve years old. And I swear I’ve never felt that about a literary tween, or if I have I was kidding myself. The honesty of the portrait hurts as Frankie hurts. I never stopped loving her and praying* for her to be safe and get through the fearful time, however horrible she was, because she was real to me, her pain was real. <i>This is what it’s really like</i>, I constantly felt. <br /><br />The relationships and interactions between Frankie and Berenice and John Henry are central. John Henry is as convincing a character as Frankie. Often he is more sympathetic: when Frankie uses him (to relieve her loneliness) or mistreats him, when he longs to go out and play with the other children. Unlike Frankie his sense of himself is healthy, but like her he is at different times both nice and cruel to her in his childish ways. Berenice, a black woman who has been married several times, is sensible, straightforward, motherly up to a point. She coaxes and scolds and warns and protects Frankie, often impatient with her but never unkind and often humouring her whims. Although Frankie is not satisfied in her need for “membership” by her relationship with Berenice, she takes Berenice’s oft-repeated anecdotes and life-stories to heart, they form the basis of her meagre knowledge about romantic love and sexuality. Her empathy for Berenice, uneven and awkwardly or rudely expressed, is redeeming; she will not always be this awful awkward twelve year old. She will grow into someone capable of loving and caring for others.<br /><br />Frankie’s wrestles with her identity and the instabilty of her self-perception are staged with the seriousness of tragedy, of the grandeur and pain with which Frankie experiences them. Her adolescent alienation, never elevated to a heroic struggle, is embodied in the sometimes disjointed and repetative language, which is also hauntingly beautiful, like a sorrowful chant. It’s the writing of a genius of feeling…</span>
  <a data-text-id="12400722546488279831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3066005880" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3066005880" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3066005880?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3066005880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5853215282682846105"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576201977i/28602323._SX540_.png' alt='middagh' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Here’s the February House in Brooklyn, one of the great artistic flophouses, where for a while in the 1940s Carson McCullers crashed and crashed with WH Auden, Jane and Paul Bowles, Gypsy Rose Lee and, briefly, a chimpanzee. McCullers was an avid drunk, as was everyone else. One night they ran out into the street to chase a fire engine, as one does, and McCullers came back with the idea for this book. Which does not, by the way, have a fire engine in it, or a chimpanzee.<br/><br/>Instead it has a twelve-y</span>
  <span id="freeText5853215282682846105" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576201977i/28602323._SX540_.png" alt="middagh" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Here’s the February House in Brooklyn, one of the great artistic flophouses, where for a while in the 1940s Carson McCullers crashed and crashed with WH Auden, Jane and Paul Bowles, Gypsy Rose Lee and, briefly, a chimpanzee. McCullers was an avid drunk, as was everyone else. One night they ran out into the street to chase a fire engine, as one does, and McCullers came back with the idea for this book. Which does not, by the way, have a fire engine in it, or a chimpanzee.<br><br>Instead it has a twelve-year-old in it. This is a real life young adult novel, written about young adults for young adults, and I definitely want to point out that why is anyone still reading Catcher in the Rye when this and We Have Always Lived in the Castle exist. If you know any teenagers, for fuck’s sake give them exactly these two books. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576201978i/28602324._SX540_.jpg" alt="Member-Of-The-Weddi-hirez-006" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>That’s Anna Paquin!</i><br><br>Anyway Frankie is twelve, so she changes her name twice over the course of the book. She spends a lot of time sitting around a table with the family cook and some creepy little kid. Seriously, that’s like three quarters of the plot, and if you think that sounds boring then you haven’t read anything by Carson McCullers, who’s a writer who not only goes “straight to the story, as a circus dog breaks through the paper hoop,” but who also wrote that sentence about it. You should start now! <br><br>“The world is certainly a sudden place,” and a dangerous one, and danger creeps up here - at first you’re like hey, that guy’s creepy, and then you’re like oh no he’s coming over here, and then...the world is a sudden place. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[She whales him in the head with a pitcher, btw, is what happens. In case you really wanted to know how much trauma you’re in for. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>McCullers had moved out of the February House by the time she finished <i>Member of the Wedding.</i> It took her five years! It’s not even a long book! But it is perfect, so there’s that. Be perfecter with a chimpanzee, I guess. Let the chimp bust through the paper hoop. Now that’s a story! But this, this too.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="5853215282682846105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_760973020" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/760973020" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/760973020?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553250510" href="/book/show/702459.The_Member_of_the_Wedding">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=500-wm">500-wm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=shorty-short">shorty-short</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2015">r-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer760973020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3447644082350866864">I'd imagine the word 'universal' gets thrown around a lot in regards to this work. The temptation of it is exactly why I am excising it from my vocabulary, for even the small amount of literature I've read in the culverts of unacknowledged canon were enough to show the lie of the word. I find an immense amount of resonance in this work, resonance structured on a foundation of tokenism, sentimentality, and other measures of self-willed isolation commonly shared with other white people works of 'u</span>
  <span id="freeText3447644082350866864" style="display:none">I'd imagine the word 'universal' gets thrown around a lot in regards to this work. The temptation of it is exactly why I am excising it from my vocabulary, for even the small amount of literature I've read in the culverts of unacknowledged canon were enough to show the lie of the word. I find an immense amount of resonance in this work, resonance structured on a foundation of tokenism, sentimentality, and other measures of self-willed isolation commonly shared with other white people works of 'universal' meaning. I do not claim that works such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5948927._____" title="التيه by Abdul Rahman Munif" rel="nofollow noopener">Cities of Salt</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52385.Almanac_of_the_Dead" title="Almanac of the Dead by Leslie Marmon Silko" rel="nofollow noopener">Almanac of the Dead</a> do not rely on the same dynamics of self vs other, but no one would think to call them universal. That epithet requires power, and the world at large is not of a mind to grant them that.<br /><br />How much does the cult of US American childhood play a part in letting millions of white parents sleep at night? Boys will be boys, girls will be sugar and sweet, and every excuse will be made when a troubled teenage soul slaughters their propagators with gun in hand. I wonder how many condemned Frankie's father for letting her roam rather than the systematic excision of her mind from her body by the mores of society. There are the usual excuses: lack of mother, lack of white female friends of a common age, the lack of urban space commonly put as the ultimate solution by the North and the South. As per usual, McCullers comes much closer to the heart of it than most who try their hand at the metaphysics of growing up, but the threat society places on the body of a young white girl is still centered around that fact of whiteness.<br /><br />I may be too old to take as deep a comfort in this as I would have once, but my methods of reacting to fear of the oncoming void with rampant imagination are no different now than they were at age twelve and under. Enough experience has honed it into a serviceable way of living in this capitalistic age, replete with the communication skills and awareness of personal strengths requisite in this country of mine. However, I now know that I am never going to "grow up"; for better or for worse.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3447644082350866864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_878741339" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/878741339" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/878741339?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/15745060-the-member-of-the-wedding">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=southern">southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=life-stages">life-stages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2014-read">2014-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=kids">kids</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer878741339" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16348839451595891951">Yes, a gem! Why I found it amazing and thus worth five stars is explained below in the partial review.<br/><br/>I will only add here a bit about the book's setting: Georgia, 1944-45. You see the world through the eyes of 12 year old Frankie, or F. Jasmine Addams. SHE, not I, will explain to you why she appropriated this name. Not only do you see the emotional turmoil of a preteen but you also get the racial tensions in the South and the tension created by the War. We know it is 1944 from the simple line t</span>
  <span id="freeText16348839451595891951" style="display:none">Yes, a gem! Why I found it amazing and thus worth five stars is explained below in the partial review.<br /><br />I will only add here a bit about the book's setting: Georgia, 1944-45. You see the world through the eyes of 12 year old Frankie, or F. Jasmine Addams. SHE, not I, will explain to you why she appropriated this name. Not only do you see the emotional turmoil of a preteen but you also get the racial tensions in the South and the tension created by the War. We know it is 1944 from the simple line that "Patton is driving the Germans out of France". One line and so much is said. No long discourses on history.<br /><br />Do you remember when you were caught between being a child and an adult and belonging nowhere? Alone....and the world is a scary place.<br /><br />The narration is fantastic; it is read slowly, with feeling, and it is easy to follow. Wonderful Southern dialect.<br /><br /><br />***********************************<br /><br /><br />After part two of three OR after three fourths of a 6 hour audiobook: <br /><br />Lend me your ear for a moment please. I consider myself pretty hard to please. For this reason I tend to prefer non-fiction because then I tell myself I will at least learn something if the writing disappoints, if the story fails. But the most stupendous books are those of fiction where the writers create a marvelous gem all from NOTHING. They create a tale from assorted words and how they string them together, their imagination and their ability to capture human emotions that we all share. So when I run into astoundingly beautiful writing, and by that I do not mean "pretty" but rather writing that speaks to us all, that has the ability to to pull us out of our own existence and allows us to share common experiences and emotions, now that is something else. THAT is what Carson McCullers does in this book. Fantastic writing. <br /><br />Do you remember your preteens, when you didn't feel comfortable in your own skin, when the whole world changed over night and all was frightening? Physical changes and emotional changes that throw you off balance. Do you really remember that period in your life? Here it is again captured in writing. <br /><br />Don't read this. Listen to it narrated by Susan Sarandon. Stunning performance. <br /><br />Don't miss this book. <br /><br />Yep, I have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37380.The_Heart_is_a_Lonely_Hunter" title="The Heart is a Lonely Hunter by Carson McCullers" rel="nofollow noopener">The Heart is a Lonely Hunter</a>. This is even better!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16348839451595891951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_400808450" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/400808450" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/400808450?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=gorgeous-prose">gorgeous-prose</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=angst">angst</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400808450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16190733902221796345">I was drawn to this story of 12-year-old Frankie, who is restless and fearful and jealous of anyone who is happy, because she is such a jumble of adolescent angst. <br/><br/>"This was the summer when Frankie was sick and tired of being Frankie. She hated herself, and had become a loafer and a big no-good who hung around the summer kitchen ... The war and the world were too fast and big and strange. To think about the world for very long made her afraid. She was not afraid of Germans or bombs or Japanese. </span>
  <span id="freeText16190733902221796345" style="display:none">I was drawn to this story of 12-year-old Frankie, who is restless and fearful and jealous of anyone who is happy, because she is such a jumble of adolescent angst. <br /><br />"This was the summer when Frankie was sick and tired of being Frankie. She hated herself, and had become a loafer and a big no-good who hung around the summer kitchen ... The war and the world were too fast and big and strange. To think about the world for very long made her afraid. She was not afraid of Germans or bombs or Japanese. She was afraid because in the war they would not include her, and because the world seemed somehow separate from herself." <br /><br />When it is announced that her brother is getting married, Frankie becomes obsessed with the idea of leaving home and going to live with him and his new bride. <br /><br />"I love the two of them so much. We'll go to every place together. It's like I've known it all my life, that I belong to be with them."<br /><br />The family cook, Berenice, tries to talk sense into Frankie, but she won't listen. Meanwhile, Frankie walks all over town telling everyone she meets that she's going to a wedding and then moving away, and she bosses around her young cousin, John Henry. The story builds until the weekend of the wedding, the outcome of which I will not spoil.<br /><br />This is the second Carson McCullers book I've read this year, the other being "The Ballad of the Sad Cafe," and I like her writing so much I plan to finish out her oeuvre.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16190733902221796345" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_869449018" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/869449018" />
    <a title="Connie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4146473-connie"><img alt="Connie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514035275p2/4146473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/869449018?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie" href="/user/show/4146473-connie">Connie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9801247-the-member-of-the-wedding">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=on-kindle">on-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=new-authors-2014">new-authors-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=favorites-i-recommend">favorites-i-recommend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=best-of-2014-5-stars">best-of-2014-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=book-club-reads">book-club-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer869449018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4206082062760088249"> The pure magic that McCullers creates with the written word makes this worth 4 stars right off the get go. She gives us the character of Frankie or F. Jasmine who is so ready to leave 12 years behind and move forward that she is a bundle of nerves and dreams. She doesn't feel like she fits in her skin any more and is so anxious to shed it and find out who she is suppose to be. That terrible angst of adolescence, the feeling that you are suppose to be doing something else, while not quite sure y</span>
  <span id="freeText4206082062760088249" style="display:none"> The pure magic that McCullers creates with the written word makes this worth 4 stars right off the get go. She gives us the character of Frankie or F. Jasmine who is so ready to leave 12 years behind and move forward that she is a bundle of nerves and dreams. She doesn't feel like she fits in her skin any more and is so anxious to shed it and find out who she is suppose to be. That terrible angst of adolescence, the feeling that you are suppose to be doing something else, while not quite sure you want to leave what is safe but knowing for certain that there is more for you in life. This is a true tale of one's coming of age. Standing right on the edge, knowing that once you step over you are never quite so carefree and innocent....but you so want to step into the grown up side. A time of excitement, fear and woe all mixed together.<br /><br /> Frankie is determined to travel the world with her brother and his soon to be bride. She is ready to leave all that she knows behind and seek adventure. She feels stifled in a small town where she doesn't fit in, isn't a member of the club and her best friends are the very wise, black housekeeper Berneice and her 6 year old cousin John Henry. She laments her fate day after day as she awaits the big wedding day, but the closer it gets the more she seems to reminisce and feels a little sad about moving on. Her dreams are big but so far her world is small even though it is safe.<br /><br /><br /> McCullers took me into that old southern house, right up to the kitchen table eating Hoppin John, playing bridge with sticky stained cards while the flies buzzed in the thick and humid air. I could feel Frankie just bubbling over to tell anyone who would listen that she was going to leave this little town behind. I could hear the regret in Berneice's voice as she told her stories, I could feel John Henry's childish wonder at everything around him.<br /><br /> The changes coming to the South ran parallel to Frankies growing up. The world was at war, Civil Rights were just around the corner and the country would never be the same again. You could feel the changes that were coming, they just hung heavy in the air....for Frankie and the South that she knew so well. Such a simple story, taking place over only a few days, but so well crafted the impression it leaves will last through time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4206082062760088249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3074587502" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3074587502" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55478778-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462205788p2/55478778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3074587502?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/55478778-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140017823" href="/book/show/2676846-the-member-of-the-wedding">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=women-usa">women-usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=20th-century-women">20th-century-women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3074587502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12708134316206143164">I absolutely loved this book right up until Part III, which is very short, a kind of addendum added onto the main story. And I thought - no: that is overkill - overkill being the apposite word. It's as if the talented Ms McCullers couldn't rest with the story just as it was - she had to make it into a rip-your-heart-out climatic ending. Maybe some people like that?<br/><br/>So, the delightful, superb, so beautiful middle section is quite simply the three main characters: Frankie, or F. Jasmine as she call</span>
  <span id="freeText12708134316206143164" style="display:none">I absolutely loved this book right up until Part III, which is very short, a kind of addendum added onto the main story. And I thought - no: that is overkill - overkill being the apposite word. It's as if the talented Ms McCullers couldn't rest with the story just as it was - she had to make it into a rip-your-heart-out climatic ending. Maybe some people like that?<br /><br />So, the delightful, superb, so beautiful middle section is quite simply the three main characters: Frankie, or F. Jasmine as she calls herself in part II, Berenice and little John Henry West, Frankie's cousin - just sitting around eating dinner - hoppin'-john (peas and rice), Frankie's favourite; sweet potatoes roasted and split open, ham on the bone, which Berenice pulls at daintily with her fingers and corn bread to mop up the pot liquor!<br /><br />It's not the food that gets me - although that is enjoyable; it is the relationships between the three and how they talk to each other - how they love each other and how the two children take all their worldly thoughts to Berenice and how Berenice relates the stories of her three bad marriages and her one good one.<br /><br />There are various minor characters in the background, Frankie's father, the soldier, friends of Berenice - Honey and T.T., the people F. Jasmine meets in her walks through town - but they just form a kind of hazy surround to those three central characters - a fourth maybe is the town itself - hot and sultry in the Deep South.<br /><br />Here is an example of McCullers catching with pitch perfect ease the conversation between the twelve year old and the house-maid, Berenice who cooks the children's meals - and does everything else too.<br /><br /><i> 'Now tell me your honest opinion,' F. Jasmine said.<br />But Berenice looked at the orange satin evening dress and shook her head and did not comment. At<br />first she shook her head with short little turns, but the longer she stared, the longer these shakes became, until at the last shake F. Jasmine heard her neck crack.<br /> 'What's the matter?' F. Jasmine asked.<br /> 'I thought you was going to get a pink dress.'<br /> 'But when I got in the store I changed my mind. What is wrong with this dress? Don't you like it Berenice?'<br /> 'No,' said Berenice. 'It don't do.'<br /> 'What do you mean? It don't do.'<br /> 'Exactly that. It just don't do.'<br /> F. Jasmine turned to look in the mirror, and she still thought the dress was beautiful. But Berenice had a sour and stubborn look on her face, an expression like that of an old long-eared mule, and F. Jasmine could not understand.<br /> 'But I don't see what you mean,' she complained. 'What is wrong?'<br /> Berenice folded her arms over her chest and said: 'Well, if you don't see it I can't explain it to you. Look there at your head, to begin with.'<br /> F. Jasmine looked at her head in the mirror.<br /> 'You had all your hair shaved off like a convict, and now you tie a silver ribbon around this head without any hair. It just look peculiar.'<br /> 'Oh, but I'm washing my hair tonight and going to try and curl it,' F. Jasmine said.<br /> 'And look at them elbows,' Berenice continued. 'Here you got on this grown woman's evening dress. Orange satin. And that brown crust on your elbows. The two things just don't mix.'</i><br /><br />And so the 'discussion' swings back and forth between the two with comments thrown in from John Henry - I would like to print it all, but it's a bit long for a review - so I'll just add on the last couple of paragraphs.<br /><br /><i> 'I think you're just not accustomed to seeing anybody dressed up,' F. Jasmine said.<br /> 'I'm not accustomed to human Christmas trees in August.'<br /> So Berenice took off the sash and patted and pulled the dress in various places. F. Jasmine stood stiff like a hat rack and let her work with the dress. John Henry had got up from his chair and was watching, with the napkin still tied around his neck.</i><br /><br />The real wrench in the story is that relationship between the little boy and Frankie - the descriptions of the six year old trying his best on all occasions to please Frankie and then defying her in frustration with her impossible demands is where the heart-break truly lies.<br /><br />I suspect that in Carson McCullers' life there was a small boy - and her completely normal childhood behaviour of viewing the boy as an appendage to her own needs - was something that probably haunted her. My point however, is that we the reader are haunted by John Henry from the second he appears and each time after when Frankie either dismisses him or demands him to come at her whim.<br /><br />The second heart of the story - of course - is the black woman's tender and loving care of the two children who are not her own. She treats each of them with a gentle seriousness - which occasionally borders on the stern. But her patience and her thoughtful consideration of how each child thinks and feels is apparent in the dialogue.<br /><br />This book is really a five star read - I just can't quite forgive McCullers for the tragic last section.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12708134316206143164" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
